B0034G4P80|4.0|I'm a relative newcomer to Marvel movies, and the comic book media genre in general. I've always heard of all the different superheroes, both DC and Marvel, but none of them ever really appealed to me. When I was younger I would watch the old Superman cartoons from the 40s. They were okay, now they seem rather cheesy. I've still never read an actual comic book. And that is exactly what Marvel Studios has done with their various outings based on different members of the Avengers team. I saw Captain America in the theater, mostly because my cousin thought it was great, and she took me to go see it. I fell in love. Then the Avengers came out. There was a ton of hype surrounding it, but I never saw it. Never really had the time, to be honest. Now that it's out on DVD I think I'll pick it up. But let's get to the point. In our home, my dad previews movies before he lets the rest of the family watch them. He finally got around to Thor when it showed up for free on Amazon Instant video. He said it was okay, so we watched it.<br /><br />I would love to call Thor a 5 star epic with no flaws, as I can say about Captain America. but simply put, there are some flaws. For one, the story has too many loose ends. I felt like the writers tried to go into too many different aspects of Thor's character and existence, and in the end they stretched themselves too thin, and basically ran out of time. I think what upset me the most was that they didn't go into the relationship of Jane and Thor enough. It was like, you never really knew what was going on between them until she randomly kisses him at the end. I felt like they spent way too much time with the drama in Asgard. They could have left out a lot of the scenes with Thor's crew of warriors, because they didn't add a whole lot to the story anyway. They could have shortened the first battle scene with the Frost Giants and the crew. I thought that they did an outstanding job on Loki, and it was interesting to watch because you never knew if he had good intentions or bad intentions, and you sort of felt sorry for him because I think he didn't even know whether he had good intentions or bad intentions. The action sequences were good, but I always felt like Thor was too good for everyone else and he was just kind of toying with them. Maybe that's how it is in the comics, but to me in this movie he seemed way too god-like. I realize he is a god, but they could have made some of the stuff a little more challenging for him. I also thought what was supposed to be the big final battle with the giant metal robot guy was a little lame. He comes in, destroys the town, kills a couple people, then Thor beats the snot out of him. I thought it was hard to understand exactly why certain things happened. Like, why was Thor exiled in the first place? Why did he go back to Asgard when the girl he loved couldn't go? Why didn't he take her with him? Why did he destroy the bridge? Why didn't he just go back to Earth when he was through with Loki? I just felt like a lot of this stuff could have been tightened up and explained better. However, I do think that they could have spent more time on how he adapted to earth and its's oddness.<br /><br />Overall, this is a good movie, and one that's worth watching, especially if you want to get a ton of backstory for the Avengers. 1594744769|3.0|Miss Peregrine's Home for Peculiar Children starts out with a fanfare of promise.<br />The strange photos dispersed throughout the story riveted my attention and, as they were intended to, pulled me into the book's pages, which, I was delighted to find, were very well written. I was captivated by Riggs's taught and incisive prose--no redundancy, no dangling modifiers, no really bad grammar, just the right word every time. And I wallowed in memorable turns of phrase that seemed to cascade off the pages: \\"plaster colonnades that seemed almost delusionally aspirational\\"; \\"the sky was turning the color of a fresh bruise\\"; \\"sheep stared at us with big leaky eyes\\"; \\"looking like a beggar evangelist in his torn black suit,\\" just to quote a few. The story content got off to a good, meaty start too with references to the WWII holocaust, which had affected the main character's grandfather.<br /><br />I was engrossed in the story, happily cruising along, even dismissing the odd lapse of originality (\\"coiled like a spring\\"), until it occurred to me that the narrator, Jacob, is not an adult looking back on events of his youth, but a teenager--a boy of sixteen going on sixty. The author may have intended the narrator of the story to be a mature Jacob, but that idea isn't made clear and in any case wouldn't make sense, for reasons I can't mention here without giving the plot away. Suffice it to say that time travel can have youthful effects.<br /><br />That was the first jolt. The second jolt came when I realized the story was not going to be unusual, or something a young adult would enjoy. I had thought the historic component would play an invigorating role in the storyline, but it didn't. The story turned out to be a children's adventure story, replete with ghouls and time travel. The trouble is most children of adventure story age--seven through eleven--won't have the vocabulary to tackle a lot of the text; they'd spend more time with their noses in a dictionary than in Riggs'sbook. Young adults can certainly deal with the sophistication of the writing, but they'll be disappointed with the adventure story.<br /><br />Now, when I picked up the book, I had no idea of what the story might be about. I did, however, expect something more original, perhaps more mysterious, perhaps creative nonfiction, but certainly more thought-provoking than monsters and time travel, and the sophisticated prose fooled me into believing the book had been written for young adults. I've rarely been so confused by a work of fiction.<br /><br />I give Miss Peregrine's Home for Peculiar Children one star for the creative touch of unusual photos, one star for masterful prose, and one star for good narrative flow.

0307949486|3.0|OK, I've watched this book to suddenly show up on the bookstore shelf, quickly occupy the Most Popular category and then slowly sunset into the bargain bin. At which point I stopped dragging my feet and actually read it.<br /><br />On the positive side, the book is very readable, the plot realistic enough, the writing style robust and engaging. If you want a crime novel that doesn't make you feel an idiot after you've read it, this one fits the bill pretty well.<br /><br />(SPOILERS AHEAD!!!)<br /><br />The book is not perfect though. The characters, so realistic and believable for the most of the narration go haywire toward the end. The main protagonist, an Aspberger syndrome afflicted girl with history of abuse and uncanny investigative skills turns into a super-hacker easily penetrating any computer system, manipulating major financials transactions and camouflaging as other people with the ease that should make James Bond cringe with envy. One of the two antagonists, who starts as a fairly believable financier, who has some skeletons in the closet, and is not particularly picky about means, turns into a quintessence of evil, doing deals with anyone from the Russian Mafia to the Colombian drug cartels, trading enriched uranium etc. All of this definitely spoils an otherwise good book.<br /><br />The other issue with the book is a constant treatment with the Author's view on the social justice. We are constantly reminded that the financial markets are merely parasites on the Swedish economy, that the big Companies are no more than a bunch of fraudsters. Same with the Author's view on what justice means. Apparently, it is perfectly acceptable to hack into other people's computers - even more so when they are bad guys. Using emails and documents obtained by these means is apparently perfectly fine as well - it's used for a good goal after all. One might argue that these views are those of the book characters and not of the Author, but they are way too explicit in the book to be ignored.<br /><br />Overall, the book is not bad - nice read for a crime novel but with its own shortcomings. Based on the developments toward the end, I have a feeling the two sequel books will not be as good but this remains to be seen.e book to be ignored.<br /><br />Overall, the book is not bad - nice read for a crime novel but with its own shortcomings. Based on the developments toward the end, I have a feeling the two sequel books will not be as good but this remains to be seen.

B007FDE4Y8|3.0|I purchased this to use with my Nex 5n and the kit 18-55mm lens (at 18mm).    Note, there is no instructions with this. This is the BEST GUIDE for this stabilizer:    [...]    Overall it was ok, and with practice you can get some good results. Would I buy this again.... No. I'd pay extra and get the Glidecam 4000HD.    Firstly I'll start with the positives and then get onto my issues:    1. Feels solid and well built. I don't think its going to be breaking anytime soon.  2. The gimble is very smooth and the turning nob is very useful.  3. Various camera attachment options.    Right, now lets start with my issues:    1. Main issue is that there is not enough incremental weights!    There is no way on this earth that you could balance it on my Sony NEx 5n, with the 3 weights supplied. Of the two options that you have one is way too light and the other way too heavy. And I'm certain other people will have the same problem with their cameras. Adjusting the angle of the arm just does not provide enough flexibility.    My understanding is that the camera and the bottom weights should be in balance. This does not mean the same. For me, I had to have around 3-4x as much weight on the top plate than at the bottom. You will have to experiment.    My solution for this was to go down to my local mitre 10 with the stabiliser and buy a big bolt, along with a big bunch of washers. I'm sorry I dont have the sizes for these but if you go down to your local hardward store I'm sure you'll find some. What I found was that there is a thread on the inside of the stabilising arm into which you can screw a large bolt.    I then weighed the bolt and washers against my camera until I found the correct number of washers to match my camera and lens. Then I added these washers to the bolt and screwed these into the bottom. This was MUCH better than the options provided by Opteka.    Now although this worked, I found that balancing was very difficult and extremely temperamental. You will have topractice a lot. I then added extra weight to the platform at the top next to my camera. For every 4 washers on top I was adding one to the bottom counterweight. This extra weight definitely enhanced the ability to balance the camera.    Therefore I would say that this works better with a heavier camera than my Sony nex 5n.    2. Very temperamental and when put down goes out of balance easily    I found that the best way to combat this was to screw the silver knobs almost tight before fine tuning the balancing. Even with them screwed tight I could still turn the balancing knobs. Then once balanced perfectly I really tighten the silver screws to ensure that there could be no movement. This was much better.    Anyway, they are my main gripes, and quite big ones. Good luck if you purchase it. I hope this helps.    Rich

B009HEP41A|4.0|Anna Karenina is a classic of Russian literature, written by the celebrated author Leo Tolstoy in 1873. It tells the story of the titular character, Anna, a Moscow socialite married to the taciturn Alexei Karenin, a stoic Government official 20 years her senior. Anna's life is thrown into turmoil when she meets and falls for the dashing Count Vronsky, a handsome and wealthy cavalry officer who sweeps Anna off her feet, and shows her the true meaning of love. However, repressive societal norms, pressure from friends and family, and Anna's own insecurities about what she wants from life means that her difficult choice between a safe, but dull life with Karenin and a wild, but potentially ostracizing life with Vronsky becomes agonizing. The story has been told on film many times over the years; this lavish new version is directed by Joe Wright from a screenplay by Tom Stoppard, stars Keira Knightley as Anna, Jude Law as Alexei, and Aaron Taylor-Johnson as Vronsky, and has a stellar supporting cast that includes Kelly Macdonald, Olivia Williams, Emily Watson and Matthew Macfadyen.<br /><br />The score for Anna Karenina is by the Anglo-Italian composer Dario Marianelli, who worked with director Wright on Pride and Prejudice in 2005 and won an Academy Award for his score for their film Atonement in 2007. As one might expect given the subject matter and the time period in which the film is set, Marianelli's score is very, very classical, and is generally built around a series of opulent waltzes. Marianelli's use of waltzes throughout the score is clearly intended to mirror the dance-like courtship and love triangle between Anna, Alexei and Vronsky, as the central figures move about between each other in an increasingly complicated web of deceit, passion and anger, fueled by Anna's continual oscillation between the two men in her life.<br /><br />There is a central recurring theme heard throughout the score - a four-note motif very similar to James Horner's danger motif that first appears in \\"Clerks\\" and is subsequently restated throughout the score, most notably in \\"Anna's Last Train\\" - but strongly identifiable thematic content is not the crux of the score in terms of it being the most memorable element. Instead, the various instrumental textures and recurring compositional styles characterize Marianelli's work; in terms of orchestration, the score relies heavily on a string and woodwind-led orchestra, with only the merest hints of brass and percussion. Like Jane Eyre before it, Anna Karenina also acts as a showcase for the violin performances of the British wunderkind Jack Liebeck, whose sumptuous solos can be heard in several cues, notably the opening \\"Overture\\", \\"Unavoidable\\", and \\"I Don't Want You To Go\\". For me, a more solid thematic core would have made the score a little more approachable, but despite this small drawback Marianelli's lavish music remains interesting for its duration.<br /><br />Cues such as \\"Anna Marches into a Waltz\\", \\"Kitty's Debut\\" and \\"Dance With Me\\" conjure up imagery of well-heeled gentlemen and beautiful women in fine gowns lavishly courting each other in glittering Romanov ballrooms. \\"Dance With Me\\", especially, is a very clever cue which becomes more frantic, dissonant and disjointed as it progresses as Anna is literally swept of her feet by Vronsky, causing her previously ordered and cautious world to be thrown into chaos.<br /><br />Russian folk music plays a major part in the score too, and to capture its authenticity Marianelli often highlights traditional local instrumentation, including balalaikas and garmon accordions. There is also a great deal of regional dance pastiche in the form of mazurka-style pieces that give the score a distinctly classic Russian flavor. \\"She Is of the Heavens\\", for example, has a section for whistlers and a lyrical vocal performance over a bold, vibrant traditional melody for clarinets, while later tracks such as \\"The Girl and the Birch\\" use new performances of an old Russian folk song called `Beroza' to excellent effect. The \\"Can Can\\" is a wild gypsy dance piece that is wonderfully anarchic, while at the other end of the scale the opera cue \\"At the Opera\\" sets Tolstoy's own words from Anna Karenina Part 2 Chapter 9 to a classically inflected orchestral melody that is very effective.<br /><br />As the score develops into its second half it becomes more serious, and more conventionally romantic, capturing both the passionate relationship between Anna and Vronsky, and the subsequent devastating fallout she suffers, both in her marriage to Karenin, and her standing in Muscovite social circles. Cues such as \\"I Don't Want You To Go\\", \\"Too Late\\" and \\"Leaving Home and Coming Home\\" give Liebeck the chance to convey assorted tortured emotions through his violin performances, while the lush and emotional \\"Lost in a Maze\\", the unexpectedly powerful \\"A Birthday Present\\", and truly sumptuous \\"I Know How To Make You Sleep\\" allows the full orchestra to rise to the fore in some of the score's few moments of overwhelming thematic beauty. It is these cues that will undoubtedly capture the attention of those who fell in love with Marianelli's earlier romance scores.<br /><br />Anyone who came to Marianelli through scores like Pride & Prejudice and Jane Eyre will find a great deal of the latter half of Anna Karenina to their liking, but your tolerance for the classic waltzes and intentionally authentic Russian folk music that makes up most of the first half of the score will determine your level of appreciation for the CD as a whole. The lack of a truly memorable main theme may make Anna Karenina a little impenetrable for some, too. Personally I found the Anna Karenina to be a richly orchestrated and beautifully performed score which is perfect for the film, which is unfortunately missing that tantalizing, elusive `something' that would otherwise result in multiple returns to the soundtrack.

1455502758|4.0|\\"Mortality\\" is a slim volume of essays written by a dying man.  Hitchens, an avowed atheist, was famously diagnosed with cancer and in a series of articles first written for Vanity Fair chronicled both his decline as well as how he became the focus of a debate over questions about God and faith.<br /><br />Never shy about skewering sacred cows, Hitchens takes on conventions such as how all those diagnosed with cancer seem to \\"bravely\\" battle on - and even questions the very concept of a \\"war on cancer\\" itself.  He discusses the debate that swirled around his illness and condition, never wavering from his atheist views, and records some of the mundane, the treatments and the fatigue related to being so gravely ill.<br /><br />Although the topics are somewhat interesting, and the discussions well written, it is the overall ordinariness of this book that is most valuable.  In the preface and afterward to the book Hitchens is lauded as an extraordinary person of inestimable talent and intellect.  It would seem, though, just as we all put on our pants one leg at a time (well, perhaps except for circus performers) we all are destined to shuck off our mortal coil, as has been said, and \\"Mortality\\" takes us briefly and superficially into that part of the human experience to which we will all be subject.  If there is comfort here, a book written by a dying atheist as he loses the things he values most - his voice, his pen, and then his life - it is that he makes the voyage almost seem ordinary, which of course is something that American society has a hard time accepting.

B004LWZWGA|3.0|Christopher Nolan's latest and last installment of his revamped Batman trilogy, The Dark Knight Rises, is arguably the film with the most heart of the trio. This is more of a cerebral film than its predecessors. You will not see as much of Batman as before, however you will see more Bruce Wayne. You will not see the level of gritty violence or drawn-out brawling that The Dark Knight. delivered. You will also, sadly, not see Heath Ledger. You will, however, see the Dark Knight rise.<br /><br />Bruce Wayne (Christian Bale or, as some websites like to call him, \\"BatBale\\") is a broken man, both mentally and physically. He is recovering from his injuries endured at the end of The Dark Knight, as well as his broken heart. When Selina Kyle (Anne Hathaway) takes him by surprise by robbing his \\"uncrackable\\" safe and stealing his mother's necklace, along with reports of Bane (Tom Hardy) causing some serious trouble in Gotham City, Bruce is inspired to once again don the mask and become the Batman.<br /><br />Alfred (Michael Caine) fears for Bruce, so he leaves Wayne Manor in an attempt to provide Bruce with some clarity. This act of tough love is immediately swallowed up in the simmering peril lurking quite literally beneath the city. Bane is about to rain more terror and brutality on the city of Gotham than we have seen it encounter thus far and, for the first time, Batman might not be enough to stop him.<br /><br />Hathaway makes an excellent Catwoman, and thankfully, because Nolan is a master of subtlety, she is never actually titled as such, nor is she dressed like a fool. She is quick, both in wit and fight, and she reinvents a character that seemed beyond saving after the crap-fest that was Halle Berry's Catwoman. Best of all, she is believable.<br /><br />Hardy's Bane is a magnetic villain. I never had much interest in the character, yet I found myself anticipating his next scene. A major theme in this film is that we should not let our emotions control us, particularly our anger. While Bruce Wayne fights his own stubborn will to accomplish this task, we can see why, like the Joker, Bane is the polar opposite of Batman. Although while the Joker may have had no other motive than to \\"watch the world burn\\", Bane's motivation is more rational and understandable. While we both loved Ledger's Joker and anticipated his demise, we find ourselves caught off-guard in that we can honestly sympathize with Bane.<br /><br />Gary Oldman is respectable once again as Commissioner Gordon, and Joseph Gordon-Levitt, an actor whose work I would always gladly watch twice, was excellent as John Blake. Gordon-Levitt is an actor who rarely, if ever, lets me down. His presence in a film is almost a guarantee that it will not suck.<br /><br />Marion Cotillard was satisfying as Miranda, although I couldn't seem to separate her from her role alongside Gordon-Levitt in Inception. The female leads in these Batman films always fall flat for me. Keep an eye out for a cameo from a character we have seen in previous films who may or may not surprise you in presence alone, but who delivers one of the funnier lines of the film.<br /><br />Oldman and Gordon-Levitt (like Bale, Morgan Freeman, Hathaway, heck every actor in this film), never fail to capture your belief in their performances. These actors don't just act; they become the characters they play. This is appreciable when a film wants to transport you to a different world and actually takes you there.<br /><br />Some have complained that The Dark Knight Rises suffers from being stretched too thin, and that it did not need to be a two hour and forty-four minute film. I will argue that this film is akin to the television series \\"Mad Men\\": it simmers for a while at a slow boil, but when it finally comes to a head, you will not regret one minute of your time spent. Much of the film is spent sitting at the edge of a seat, knowing something explosive is coming yet unsure of when.<br /><br />One of the selling pointsfor me to see this film, as if I needed one, was that since I had not seen it on opening weekend, everyone I knew had already seen it and told me that the last few scenes made the movie. They were correct. I never thought I'd cry at a Batman film, and that is not only a testament to how solidly Nolan can build a foundation, but how he can also properly finish what he started. B0071XO8RA|5.0|First, let's set things straight - despite the title, this is no story of sheep.  Perhaps the greatest failing of this book series is the unassuming name which almost drove me away from it.  Don't make that mistake.<br /><br />Wool is easily one of the most compelling stories I have read in recent memory.  It's something that almost defies description, despite borrowing heavily from other tropes and themes.  Despite this, Howey manages to make something truly amazing.  A piece of post-apocalyptic narrative that just makes sense and draws you into the books.  Without going overboard, Howey delivers a narrative that is both gripping on the surface and still manages to carry depth in meaning and philosophy without being either needlessly obtuse nor boorishly brash.  It simply percolates up from the deepest mechanisms of the story in fresh pieces that can catch you by surprise.<br /><br />I wouldn't call Wool a perfect narrative, to be fair - it has stumbling blocks here and there.  Minor continuity errors or editing misses.  What makes these stand out is how few they are in relation to the overall size and scope of the work.<br /><br />If anything else needs to be said about Wool, I will say this - it is undervalued.  I wouldn't stack it next to the literary greats easily, but it certainly outshines your typical offering and is certainly of greater impact than its price might suggest.<br /><br />If you are a fan of post-apocalyptic narrative; of socio-political fiction; of sociological psychology; or a fan of Fallout - do not pass this (these) book(s) up.

0812984412|4.0|The book consists of six totally different stories--different in time, place, dialect, format, and with (more or less) totally different characters. There is, for example, the personal diary of an American notary traveling across the South Pacific in the mid-1800s, as well as the interview with a genetically-engineered clone fast-food waitress in a future dystopian Korea as she awaits the death penalty for her alleged crimes. Aren't those two things in one book by themselves enough to spark anyone's curiosity? Worked for me!<br /><br />But I don't think the appeal of this book is as wide as it may seem.<br /><br />Expecting all the stories to blend together in some cool, genius fashion, or at least for one to conclude rather naturally into the next, I was surprised at the way one story would cut off abruptly, while the next provided only a few vague allusions to the tale which had so recently immersed me in nearly overwhelming detail. And, as each section was so radically different in topic and style, some of the stories were naturally more interesting to me than others--although they were all engaging enough to keep me turning pages. (That's what really gets me. The six stories are SO different! It's hard to imagine they were written by a single author, because it's like he took on four or five different genres, and he did them all really well.)<br /><br />So on the one hand, you can consider this book quite simply as six short stories. Just ignore the fact that they're organized into a mirror format, and that the author has sewn in a few threads of continuity here and there. Those are gimmicks to justify his collection of stories as a single book, that's all. Maybe he had a lot of half-finished projects on his computer and decided to slap them together one day, and that's how Cloud Atlas was born. Why not? So if you like well-written short stories of various genres, and won't expect them all to make sense together, this book may appeal to you.<br /><br />But that wouldn't be the point, would it?<br /><br />So on the OTHER hand, you can open yourself up to consider the deeper themes suggested by Cloud Atlas. Is the uniquely-juxtaposed sum more significant than its parts? What is that significance?<br /><br />Well, it's not as though some grand, unifying meta-narrative materialized from within, not at any point during the reading process. For me, it was precisely the subtlety of the themes and connections in Cloud Atlas that made it linger so in my mind. And as it has lingered, I've come to appreciate the book more and more. It really is helpful to imagine the novel as a musical composition (as the book itself not-even-subtly encourages us to do)--we don't expect comprehensive answers to all our \\"whys\\" and \\"hows\\" from a piece of music, but rather connect to it emotionally, stepping away from its performance with an almost incommunicable understanding of the composer's passion and impetus, an understanding which cannot be reduced to the clarinet solo alone, even if that was our favorite part.<br /><br />This book can cause you to reflect on power and helplessness, human nature and society, time and reality.<br /><br />When you step back and look at our human existence, it seems so cruel, chaotic and meaningless; individuals and their fights seem trivial amidst the transience of all things. But if you step a little closer, you will see subtle connections between everything and everyone. The stories we read, the things we believe in, and the inherent indefatigable nature of humanity--these reach beyond our mere chapters of existence to compose a great work, both of and beyond our time, if not our imaginations as well. That is not just the reality in Cloud Atlas--that is our reality. And it was as if my reading the novel were a part of the novel itself. Maybe not a very interesting part, but, hey, that's how it goes.<br /><br />I would recommend this book only to people who enjoy both conventional AND unconventional fiction. For maximum reading pleasure, one must open oneself up to the subtle nature of this book. It's not the sort of postmodern fiction that will have your mind doing backflips and questioning \\"WAS THAT REAL?\\" all the way through. It's more dry and straightforward than that. But it's not so straightforward that you should take its odder elements at face value. I think that would leave you bitterly disappointed.aximum reading pleasure, one must open oneself up to the subtle nature of this book. It's not the sort of postmodern fiction that will have your mind doing backflips and questioning \\"WAS THAT REAL?\\" all the way through. It's more dry and straightforward than that. But it's not so straightforward that you should take its odder elements at face value. I think that would leave you bitterly disappointed.

0547884591|5.0|I preordered Tim Ferriss' \\"Four Hour Chef.\\" I was looking forward to the read. I'll confess--as much of a motivational rock star as Tim is, I thought I had him beat in the kitchen. Still, I was curious--Tim's claims to use cooking as a vehicle for teaching about life peaked my curiosity.  Everything he's put out has been well worth my time thus far, so even if it was for the pedagogy alone, I was in.<br /><br />I was humbled on all accounts.<br /><br />This book exceeds expectations in so many ways--First, its sheer size. I know they say that size doesn't matter, but in this case, it does. Tim has included information on every culinary style and method to which I've been exposed, and several I'm not sure I wish to be. It's a veritable compendium of all things culinary, from making a simple but elegant salad to dressing a deer. And when I say \\"dressing\\" I'm not talking about a fashion show here--Tim gets down and dirty hunting his own dinner.<br /><br />Tim's helpful hints include opening a wine bottle using a shoe--a position I've never been in before, but thanks in part to Tim's expert diagrams and illustrations, it's something I can add with pride to my skill-set.<br /><br />This might be the only R rated cookbook I've ever read--It's not one I'll hand over to my five-year old while we cook together and bond; it's adult, and it's honest--food is sensual and meat doesn't grow at the grocery store.<br /><br />In fact, that's one of the themes I most enjoyed about this book--the premise that we are at a critical junction in our food production in history. As large-scale industry swallows up small farms, we further endanger our culinary biodiversity.  Tim's goal is for us to maintain healthy, well-sourced foods and to be educated about them. He started this journey at Slow Food movement founder Alice Waters' Chez Panisse, and ended it with a culinary tour of the world.<br /><br /> \\"Four Hour Chef\\" is well organized with detailed sections ranging from basic skills andequipment to techniques of the pros. Tim includes a chefography and research bibliography, and has a helpful reference section in the end. There are hundreds of world-renown chefs in \\"Four Hour Chef,\\" many of whom Tim has traveled to meet. He has addressed many culinary styles: fresh local food chefs, the French greats, gastronomes and even field chefs. He has also included a section about the science behind it all.<br /><br />I expected to learn a lot about the art of learning but found myself delighted with Tim's ability to become a \\"master chef\\" in short order and to spin tales that made me want to be become better myself.  He proved once again that \\"material beats method\\" and that we truly can master anything.<br /><br />Well done, Tim.  And I'm not talking about the steak.

B009E3EWPI|5.0|On Dec. 10, 2007, Led Zeppelin reunited---with late drummer John Bonham's son Jason stepping in for his Dad---for a very special one-off concert at the 02 Arena in London in honor of the late great head of Atlantic Records, Ahmet Ertegun, who was a strong father figure to the band. Only 16,000 super-lucky fans were able to get tickets out of the millions who applied. By all accounts, Zeppelin's comeback concert was a smashing success, with the band at the top of their game, and a truly magnificent chapter in rock history. Thank God then that the Led Zeppelin reunion show was filmed & recorded....but it went unreleased for 5 years.<br /><br />But now, in 2012, here it is at last: Led Zeppelin's \\"Celebration Day\\". Having just finished watching the DVD of the concert, I was absolutely blown away by how fantastic Robert Plant, Jimmy Page, John Paul Jones and Jason Bonham were in this show. The band are pure rock 'n'roll thunder, working together like a four-headed rock beast. The band blast through a 16-song, 2 hour set that is nothing short of spellbinding. From the opener \\"Good Times Bad Times\\" to the final encore of \\"Rock And Roll\\", Zeppelin are simply on fire. Plant's voice may not be *as* strong as it once was in his Zeppelin heyday, but the man can still belt it out with gusto, and he can still fire off some surprisingly strong high notes (as he does in \\"Kashmir\\", to name one example). Jimmy Page's hair may now be pure white, but he is still a phenomenal rock guitarist (and you will be cheering him on when he finally pulls out the violin bow!). John Paul Jones remains a pure powerhouse on bass and keyboards, and Jason Bonham makes his father John proud by channeling his late dad with sensational drumming prowess and skill. Like his dad, Jason can hit those drums *really hard*! Somewhere, John is smiling down on his son.<br /><br />Like many other Led Zeppelin fans, I too wish the band would do a full-on reunion tour and play more shows....but I ain't gonna hold mybreath for it. The three veteran Zeppelin men are older now and well into their 60's (with Page pushing towards 70 at the time I write this), Plant still sings great (as evidenced by this 2007 concert) but nonetheless can't *quite* sing as phenomenally as he once did (which may explain why the band did not perform \\"The Immigrant Song\\"---I'm not sure Plant can do that siren battle cry on the song anymore), and they don't really *need* to do anything else, the band's place in rock history being more than secure. And let's face it: this 02 Arena concert is SO good, how can Zeppelin ever top it? All of this is probably why a lengthy Zeppelin reunion tour is unlikely to ever be in the cards. Still...I'd love to be proven wrong!<br /><br />If \\"Celebration Day\\" really is Led Zeppelin's last stand, then they have gone out on top of the world. Let's just be grateful then that they recorded the show, and are letting us, the fans, finally get our hands on it. What a wonderful gift to Led Zeppelin fans everywhere this reunion concert is. \\"Celebration Day\\" is a MUST-OWN for Zeppelin fans. It's a brilliant final chapter for one of rock's most important bands. \\"Thank You\\" to the almighty gods of rock, Led Zeppelin!

0316228532|4.0|Like many other readers, I was delighted to hear awhile back that JK Rowling had an adult novel for us...adult like it was written for adults, not that it was going to be some erotic romance or anything like that.  I preordered a copy the day it became available, and lamented the fact that I couldn't pick it up as soon as it arrived, since I was traveling overseas at the time.  Meanwhile, as reviews started popping up around the internet, I was disappointed to read that people seem to have quite mixed opinions about this novel.<br /><br />After reading the entire thing, I can see why people have these mixed feelings.  This novel is nothing like Harry Potter in the story elements, the chief among them being that this is set in the actual world without any magic, no true heroes or villains, and no one overwhelmingly charming or likeable.  In fact, I would go so far as to say that the only character in this novel who isn't mildly repulsive in some way is our deceased gentleman, Barry.<br /><br />With all that in mind, if you only liked the Harry Potter series because of the magic and all that, this novel won't be your kind of thing.  Rowling breaks any mold we may have put her in, shows us that she's not afraid to tackle evil in the real world, and really demonstrates what a remarkable writer she is.  If nothing else, it took me at least four or five HP books before I shed some tears, but this one had me going by the end.<br /><br />Rather than telling us a story about good versus evil, this novel is about life.  Regardless of where you're from, you can probably recognize something of your life in one or more of the characters, whether that's the unhappy/happy marriage, the singledom with or without a child, neglect and abuse, going beyond expectations to break the stereotype of your life, or just being an average teenager.  The teens in this novel aren't without their flaws either: they are cruel, confused, destructive, and trying to find their way to adulthood.  Oh, and most of them hate their parents.  Shocker!<br /><br />I feel like I can't review this without at least mentioning to y'all that there are some difficult situations, and if you've got triggers for things you can't read, this might hit them.  There's abuse, both emotional and physical.  A lot of talk about drug use and what it's like for an addict trying hard to stay clean.  There are many instances of bullying and the self-injury that can result from that.  This book really does not pull any punches when it comes to these hard truths of life, and shows us what can lie behind closed doors inside any of our communities.  But if these situations are hard for you to read, I would say be warned that they exist.<br /><br />In some ways, this was an exhausting read, but I mean that in the best way.  I'm glad I read it, and I'm happy to have it on my shelf, yet I won't be picking it up again for awhile.  What I can say is that I'm very excited to see what Rowling comes up with next, and that people who enjoy good writing or character studies will enjoy this one.and most of them hate their parents.  Shocker!<br /><br />I feel like I can't review this without at least mentioning to y'all that there are some difficult situations, and if you've got triggers for things you can't read, this might hit them. What I can say is that I'm very excited to see what Rowling comes up with next, and that people who enjoy good writing or character studies will enjoy this one.

1439102813|4.0|My grandmother died of Alzheimer's - her first symptoms appeared when she was in her early 50's and she was diagnosed at 55. On the one hand, it is a great film which shows the Stones at work finalizing their famous song, Sympathy for the Devil. This being a Godard film, the action in the film will break to show something seemingly completely unrelated. It is the job of the viewer to harmonize the two and make sense of the message the great director is intending to convey.<br /><br />The following is a rough summary of the film itself. It is not intended to be a spoiler, because, being a Godard film, it must be experienced and viewed to be fully appreciated. By referring to this rough guide, some sense of the entire film may be made easier.<br /><br />First, there are the opening credits.<br /><br />Then the camera pans to a hand-painted sign which says, I believe, \\"The Rolling Stones,\\" in anagram.<br /><br />The camera cues to the Stones in rehearsal. Mick Jagger is singing an obvious demo of the lyrics, with the camera panning to Keith Richards, then Bill Wyman, Nicky Hopkins, and Charlie Watts in the background.<br /><br />Cue to a hand painted writing on the screen saying \\"Hilton Stair,\\" then activity in a hotel room.<br /><br />Cue to the Stones in rehearsal again.<br /><br />Cue to a Scene of a store, the Trenchman.<br /><br />Cue to the Stones in rehearsal.<br /><br />Cue to a Hand painted sign, \\"Outside Black Novel,\\" showing a someone reading from a book, apparently about black empowerment, in an auto wrecking yard.<br /><br />Cue to a Hand painted sign, \\"Sights and Sounds,\\" with the letters \\"SDS\\" in black letters, the rest in red.<br /><br />Cue to the Stones in rehearsal, the camera using extended panning on each Stone.<br /><br />Cue to a Hand painted sign, \\"All About Eve,\\" where a reporter with a camera asks questions, some thought-provoking, others silly, to a young woman in a forest.<br /><br />Cue to a Hand painted sign, \\"Hi FI Ction Science,\\" with the words \\"ONE\\" in black letters and the rest in red.<br /><br />Cue to the Stones in rehearsal. The rehearsal breaks abruptly to a person spray-painting a car with the following word-gram of the words, \\"Mao\\" and \\"Art.\\"<br /><br />Cue back to the Stones in rehearsal.<br /><br />Cue to a hand-painted sign, \\"Hi FI Ction Science.\\"<br /><br />Cue back to the Stones in rehearsal. The song is coming along to its recognizable, familiar sound.<br /><br />Cue to a hand-painted sign, \\"1 Plus 1 makes 2,\\"spray-painted on a wall of the wharf, the rhythm tack of Sympathy for the Devil in the background.<br /><br />Cue back to the Stones in rehearsal.<br /><br />Cue to a hand-painted sign, \\"Black Inside Syntax,\\" where a black interviewee is being asked questions about Black Power and revolution. The interview continues to the auto wrecking yard.<br /><br />Cue to a hand-painted sign, \\"Changes in SoCIAty.\\" A reference, of course, to the CIA.<br /><br />Cue back to the Stones in rehearsal.<br /><br />Cue to a hand-painted sign which is, this reviewer believes, a cross of two messages, \\"The Stones,\\" and \\"On the Beach.\\" There is gunfire and fighting on the beach.  Closing credits feature the finished song in the background.<br /><br />As Godard films go, this is more coherent than most. The Sixties, particularly 1968, when Beggars Banquet, the album which opens with Sympathy for the Devil, was being recorded. There were student demonstrations in London and Paris and the United States. These demonstrations were definitely on Godard's mind when he presented the hand painted sign which empathized the letters, \\"SDS,\\" standing for the Students for a Democratic Society, one of the many student groups existing at that time. They were politically charged times, and the energy and urgency of those times are reflected in the political messages found in the many cues from the Stones rehearsals.<br /><br />The great treat is seeing the Stones in the recording studio. Thedynamics of the group can be clearly seen in the portions of the film showing the recording of the song. The Glimmer Twins, Jagger and Richards, had clearly formed a prominent role in the group, and Brian Jones, once the leader of the group, was detached and acted uninvolved in the progression of the song. One of the most talented members of the group, he was clearly on automatic pilot. He died six months after the film was released.<br /><br />The film deserves five stars if not for a glimpse of how the Stones recorded in the studio and is one of the finer Godard films. 159420411X|3.0|I admit that I probably expected too much of this book. I thought it was a sort of accessible, wide-ranging exposition and analysis of applied probability and inference in everyday and professional problems. It is not. The topic of this book opened the possibility for deep and far reaching discussions on the nature of forecasting in fields in which randomness appears in different ways. It doesn't. So, what does this book offer? Although there are a few interesting passages, as the examples using Bayes rule to assess practical situations, most of the book is a loose and fairly uninteresting recollection of anecdotes.<br />This book needed a lot of editing and fact checking before being released. It screams for a thorough proofreading as there are many typos, misspellings and errors (it was too much for me to see that figures 8.4 and 8.6, which supposedly represent a 1000 element matrix, drawn to visually convey the image of relative frequency, are in fact 25 x 50 = 1250 element matrices!). Certain crucial characterizations (like the male lead falling in love with the main character) as explained as a matter of course. They meet and talk a few times, then we're supposed to believe that Lukas is madly in love with Jules to the point of risking his career and life. That's one example. Jules is also a disappointment; a beautiful (of course) strong-willed independent woman, who becomes bowled over emotionally based on one crush. Annoying. Aside from that stuff, the plot itself also ceases to make sense at many points. The main villain floats around poisoning and hanging people whenever he wants and nobody notices or cares, major plot elements like a \\"case\\" Jules helped solve are left completely vague, entire chunks of the story are introduced quickly and become difficult to care about (I found myself skimming through the uprising chapters). Lukas is kept in IT and not allowed to leave (ok, understandable) but they couldn't figure out a way to get him to a shower for weeks? That's kind of weird, right? Almost as if the writer was too uninspired to figure out another good way to set up conflict. And at many points the dialogue reads like a screenplay and not like how actual people talk. To be fair, nobody in the story is annoying to the point where I threw down the book in disgust, but they aren't exactly memorable either. The main baddy, who I wanted to be very bad, was just kind of boring and dense and inconsistent. And that made me sad.<br /><br />Oh, and the metaphors are super heavy-handed. The sections with Shakespeare quotes preceding them were painfully blunt.<br /><br />There are some good points as well: I like the metaplot, and the descriptions of silo life are adequately moody and evocative. An underwater scene at the end is genuinely terrifying, good job. There was obviously a lot of thought put into the layering, though from a practical standpoint I am fairly sure a silo couldn't function (in real life) (I will suspend disbelief for the sake of his interesting worldbuilding). I can see Howey taking the story a long way, which is good for him I suppose, and for more invested readers. It's really not that terrible of a book, but I feel it's been severely overrated for what it really is, which is a pretty good early novel. Not the masterpiece it's somehow been disguised as by reviewers.<br /><br />The price has also dropped a few bucks from when I bought it, so I'd say yes to picking it up for a quick read, but not if you're looking for something groundbreaking. If you want to read a good, thrilling book about interesting people living in close quarters, maybe a work from a more experienced author, like Vinge's Deepness in the Sky, would be a better choice.made me sad.<br /><br />Oh, and the metaphors are super heavy-handed. An underwater scene at the end is genuinely terrifying, good job. There was obviously a lot of thought put into the layering, though from a practical standpoint I am fairly sure a silo couldn't function (in real life) (I will suspend disbelief for the sake of his interesting worldbuilding). It's really not that terrible of a book, but I feel it's been severely overrated for what it really is, which is a pretty good early novel. B0087ITC56|3.0|That Denzel Washington is a movie star of the first rank is proven once again in Robert Zemeckis' 2012 crisis-driven character study, a film that starts out very strong but then turns into something of a morality play about addiction and personal accountability. Despite the slowdown in the narrative momentum, the actor lends a chilling honesty and his considerable charisma to his textured portrayal of Whip Whitaker, an ace commercial pilot for a regional Southern airline whose debilitating alcoholism forces him to live in a world of denial and distrust. He's middle-age, divorced, and estranged from his son, but he can still party hard with a sexy flight attendant and fly a jet airliner the morning after with the help of a line of cocaine. Their 9AM flight to Atlanta faces severe turbulence upon take-off, but it's nothing Whip can't handle with his skillful bravado intact. Having achieved momentary calm, he falls asleep at the controls after downing a couple of airline-size bottles of vodka mixed with orange juice. Suddenly the jet loses its hydraulics and descends uncontrollably with its engines on fire.<br /><br />To the shock of his by-the-book co-pilot, Whip decides to invert the plane and fly it upside down to achieve some stability in order to maneuver a last-ditch attempt at an emergency landing in an open field populated with a congregation of Jehovah's Witnesses. Miraculously, only six of 102 passengers and crew perish upon impact, and Whip is hailed as a hero much like Sully Sullenberger after he heroically saved a jet on the Hudson River. This exacting sequence is as thrilling as they come in the movies, and Zemeckis ([[ASIN:B0026O475M Cast Away]]) brings a propulsive realism to what must have seemed like a fantastical idea in John Gatins' screenplay. What follows this life-changing incident is far less unique as it becomes clear that Whip's blood alcohol count was well over the legal limit during the flight, and the ensuing investigation could easily destroy his career and send him to prison on manslaughter charges. He gets help from former colleague Charlie Anderson, the pilots' union representative for the airline, as well as Hugh Lang, a severely humorless lawyer who is pushing hard to discredit the toxicology reports which prove Whip's unfit condition for flying. The ongoing public pressure of the case sends Whip into isolation at his late father's farm where he begins another downward spiral into alcohol.<br /><br />He gets involved with recovering heroin addict Nicole whose own story of desperation is juxtaposed with his until they meet at the hospital and enter into a relationship. All this leads to a climactic hearing with the National Transportation Safety Board where his fate will be determined. Washington is onscreen for nearly the film's entire 139-minute running time, and his impassioned performance is fiercely without vanity. He is unafraid to show Whip's pathetic denial of accountability and brings a palpable face to his character's substance abuse. The supporting cast is solid with Don Cheadle lending a calm Machiavellian spirit to Lang and Bruce Greenwood a stalwart presence as unconditionally supportive Charlie. British actress Kelly Reilly ([[ASIN:B000ETRCN0 Mrs. Henderson Presents]]) sports a convincing Southern accent, but I found her a bit too languid as Nicole. In pure scene-stealing mode, John Goodman plays Whip's unapologetic drug dealer Harling Mays like he was redoing Walter Sobchak in [[ASIN:B000I9WW0E The Big Lebowski]]. It's a comically anomalous turn but something of a relief especially during the sturm-und-drang of the film's second half. Speaking of which, Zemeckis and Gatins' unsubtle storytelling approach resuscitates all-too-familiar scenes of alcohol abuse that make the story feel more predictable than it had to be as the inevitable events play themselves out. Regardless, Washington makes the film eminently watchable.roy his career and send him to prison on manslaughter charges. Washington is onscreen for nearly the film's entire 139-minute running time, and his impassioned performance is fiercely without vanity. He is unafraid to show Whip's pathetic denial of accountability and brings a palpable face to his character's substance abuse. The supporting cast is solid with Don Cheadle lending a calm Machiavellian spirit to Lang and Bruce Greenwood a stalwart presence as unconditionally supportive Charlie. British actress Kelly Reilly ([[ASIN:B000ETRCN0 Mrs. Henderson Presents]]) sports a convincing Southern accent, but I found her a bit too languid as Nicole. In pure scene-stealing mode, John Goodman plays Whip's unapologetic drug dealer Harling Mays like he was redoing Walter Sobchak in [[ASIN:B000I9WW0E The Big Lebowski]]. It's a comically anomalous turn but something of a relief especially during the sturm-und-drang of the film's second half. Speaking of which, Zemeckis and Gatins' unsubtle storytelling approach resuscitates all-too-familiar scenes of alcohol abuse that make the story feel more predictable than it had to be as the inevitable events play themselves out. Regardless, Washington makes the film eminently watchable.

B007THA4FI|4.0|J. K. Rowling's novel, The Casual Vacancy, is a very good British village story that captures the atmosphere of a setting US readers may never experience directly. There is a long history of novelists writing in this tradition, one that comes to mind immediately is Simon Brett whose novels involve local mystery stories. I enjoy this style because of the detailed descriptions of the characters and their interactions. A key to this type of fiction is the sharply drawn images of individual emotions of the characters. The presentation of the emotional reactions of the characters is precise, and it is clear to the reader exactly what the characters are going through. This is very difficult to do, and Rowling writes with insight demonstrating a mastery of the technique.<br /><br />The story of the sudden death of a councilman in the small town of Pagford and the politics of a variety of personalities attempting to continue the council's agenda is enjoyable. I like the structure of the novel, like a spiral that starts with a broad circumference and continues in narrower bands as the novel progresses. Characters are drawn from the mundane routines of their daily lives into the vortex of interaction involving the troubling issues of family problems, jealousy, envy, snobbery, poverty, racism, drug abuse, encroaching public housing, hopelessness, child abuse, self-mutilation, displacement, rehabilitation, and possibly fleeing Pagford for a location with less personal involvement with society. The tightening swirl of the action forces all of the important characters to make decisions that they once thought were not required in an idyllic British village.<br /><br />Although The Casual Vacancy is certainly no British cozy, the reader will love the village and the people (some more than others) who are drawn into the spin of upsetting life events precipitated by the death of a charismatic town fixer. In the end, you will be forced to make decisions like the characters to accept the responsibilities and commitment of a local community or flee to the much less connected life of larger cities. There were a few parts of the novel (within a few scenes) in which the thread of the narrative was a bit stretched if not broken, so I gave it 4 stars instead of 5.he responsibilities and commitment of a local community or flee to the much less connected life of larger cities. There were a few parts of the novel (within a few scenes) in which the thread of the narrative was a bit stretched if not broken, so I gave it 4 stars instead of 5.

0307887448|5.0|Ready Player One is set in the near future, when mankind has pretty much given up on preserving the Earth and now spends most of its time in OASIS, a virtual world.  Wade Watts attends a virtual high school and is fixated, like most people, on one thing: winning a challenge set by the now-dead James Halliday.  Halliday created OASIS and became the richest man alive, and before he died he created a contest: to find three keys hidden deep in OASIS.  The first to complete the puzzle will win his fortune.  Wade is poor, lives in a trailer, and is lucky to have the basic equipment he needs to log into OASIS.  Without resources he can't possibly travel to the many worlds inside OASIS to look for the keys.  What he can do, though, is learn everything he possibly can about Halliday's history and favorite things, which all come from Halliday's childhood during the eighties.<br /><br />Unlike Halliday, and clearly Ernest Cline, I didn't like the eighties, and I don't have warm fuzzy memories of John Hughes movies, Cyndi Lauper, arcade games, etc.  So I thought I wouldn't enjoy this book, but I did.  Maybe because the eighties pop references are so integrated into a fantastic story.  Maybe because everything in this book was so intricately detailed, I couldn't help but be impressed.  Reading this book felt like you were part of the puzzle, because everything in it triggers a memory of something you forgot you knew.  Cline doesn't go for the super-obvious, but for the slightly, more obscure references, like the movie WarGames.<br /><br />If this book was just one long collection of pop culture references, I'd have gotten bored pretty quickly.  But it's not.  Wade is competing against all the other people trying to win this prize, and he's also competing against the evil corporation, IOI, which has employed people (the soulless Sixers) who spend their days trying to win this contest so that OASIS can end up under corporate control.  Wade and the other contestants are fighting to keep IOI from ruining their beloved virtual world.  They are working alone but you know they'll have to team up to share resources if they are going to beat all the money of IOI.<br /><br />If it sounds a little formula, it is. But the twist on the solve-the-puzzle, fight-corporate-greed story is this: it's mostly happening in a virtual world.  The cool thing about this book is that Cline really thinks about how his story goes in and out of the real world, and what it means if in the future, most of our interactions are through avatars rather than in-person.<br /><br />This book isn't Shakespeare, by a long shot.  Cline is either ripping everyone from the 80's off or he's completely brilliant. This is what I call a \\"real time case study\\" and it convinced me to read this biography.<br /><br />The truly interesting aspect of this biography is that it was completed while Jobs was alive and most of his compatriots, friends/co-workers/enemies, were alive with fresh memories to share. I don't think the book sugar coats anything.  In fact, I feel Isaacson dwells too much on the bizarre aspects of Jobs nature. Oddly though you do gradually get a sense that certain - not most - of these disturbing sides of Jobs nature are in fact an integral part of his creativity/greatness.  The misleading aspect of focusing too much on Jobs spectacular oddities is that it tends to override the unique talents that Jobs displayed to instantly grasp the full landscape of an infant technology and recognize how to elegantly implement it for public consumption.  He doesn't need to create the infant technology and rarely does.  Reading the book in this light Isaacson provides sufficient instances of Jobs unique ability to spot great ideas and understand there complete future in a flash.  Not only did he see these futures in a flash but he knew how to translate that vision to his work force.  This ability coupled with an obsession for details/quality and unyielding demand for creativity from his workforce allowed him to implement his visions while also fulfilling the aspirations of the core of his workforce - a powerful and rarely accomplished feat.  Even with all his harshness toward his workforce, they learned to deal with that and realized/appreciated his ability to raise their own creative ability to unexpected levels. Don't we all want this out of our life?<br /><br />I have always been curious as to why organizations blossom, mature and decline even though they have all the financial and personnel advantages available to them at their height.  Jobs knew how to prevent this from happening and demonstrated that he could reverse the trend when he returned to Apple.  He could be brutal but it was the only way he knew to prevent \\"the bozos\\" from taking control of the company.<br /><br />So, Isaacson provides enough insights to allow one to glean where the exceptional greatness of Jobs sprang from.  It was not all goodness and kindness but don't be overwhelmed with these aspects of Jobs nature.  Look for the traits of his nature that made friends and co-workers idolize him - Isaacson provides numerous examples of these traits.<br /><br />Enjoy the finer less splashy aspects of the book.

B005NPCBI6|5.0|A Momentary Lapse of Reason was the first album for the group following the acrimonious split with writer, singer and bass player, Roger Waters.  Many perhaps feared that the band would falter without Waters who provided so much of the band's lyrical direction and album concepts. This album however, proved that Pink Floyd was not only alive and well, but that it would remain one of the most successful and popular bands of all-time.  It illustrated that Dave Gilmour is not only a phenomenal musician, but an intelligent businessman.<br /><br />The album is definitely more contemporary and accessible to the average listener compared to earlier works with songs like Learning to Fly and On the Turning Away being huge hits for the band. Yet while there is a gulf in tone between the previous Final Cut, the group has lost none of its musical technical brilliance.  Combined with Gilmour's soulful guitar work that is comfortable playing call-and-response in Learning to Fly, or driving the song in Sorrow, all the elements that make Pink Floyd worth listening to are alive and well.<br /><br />The concept album is still there and they immerse you straight away with Signs of Life, an eerie musical intro' in which you're listening to a rower pull their way down a waterway, a format that was rekindled in The Division Bell. The you're thrust into the uplifting Learning to Fly kicks you in a new directon.<br /><br />There definitely is less of the brooding atmosphere and cynical intellectual turmoil that Waters enjoyed exploring and was not everyone's cup of tea, but it's not all gone.  A New Machine parts I and II sees Gimour's voice enhanced by synthesizer and is especially haunting and forboding. And of course the last song, Sorrow, something of Floydian classic, which knocks away any chance you thought the band was all pop TV friendly.  The opeing guitar work was apparently recorded in an ice hockey rink with rotating amps blasting out Gilmour's thick, mournful notes.<br /><br />Definitely one of my favorite elements for it's richness of sound.  I love the instrumentals and yet in plenty of the songs there are snippets of throwbacks to previous floyd sound techniques found in earlier albums, with the use of electronic sounds and the spoken human voice. Buy a decent set of headphones for this one.

0547564651|5.0|Intelligence will only get you so far in life. This is probably obvious to anyone who has known (or been!) an under-achiever or has known someone who succeeded despite modest intelligence. But author Paul Tough goes a step further in explaining why this is -- and what other -- malleable -- traits help lead to success. He looks at how optimism, responsibility, curiosity, and most importantly grit -- help people succeed, and how experiences at both ends of the socioeconomic scale derail people from fulfilling their potential. A lack of love and support early in life leads to hopelessness, poor impulse control, and a slew of life-long negative effects. But a lack of challenge and experience of success-despite-previous-failure makes people risk-adverse and gritless.<br /><br />In other words, the findings Tough presents have repercussions for a sizable slice of the population -- from the resource-poor to helicopter parents. This book appealed to me as a parent, a citizen interested in education policy, and a 30-something interested in understanding more about my development, as well as my peer's. Tough presents a series of studies and anecdotes that show what matters and why, and suggest possible interventions to strengthen children's characters.<br /><br />In his examination of what makes great chess players great, Author Tough mentions people's tendency towards confirmation bias -- looking at data in the way that is most supportive to their original position and ignoring data that is contradictory and doesn't fit. I fear that, in reporting on the numerous studies in various disciplines, the author has fallen to the same malaise -- biochemical research is rarely cut and dry, and social, psychological, and economic research even less so. In other words, I suspect the truth about the power of grit, optimism, etc. is much more complicated and confounding than Tough makes it out to be -- and the potential solutions to increasing success likewise more convoluted. That should not diminish from the value of this book, however, which presents important information we should all consider in thinking about how to shape the path towards our children's future.

159420411X|2.0|I'm a member of SABR, a fan of Silver's political blog, and as an atmospheric science professor I teach seminars on forecasting (weather forecasting and also an interdisciplinary seminar in prediction).  I've won awards in a national weather forecasting contest, too.  I thought this book would be the long-awaited grand unifier of the field of prediction.  Instead, any time that Silver turns from his areas of expertise toward science, he faceplants.<br /><br />Chapter 4: Nate thinks the U.S. weather forecast models are run on computers in Boulder, CO--completely wrong, total confusion of a research lab with the operational forecasting center in Maryland.  He doesn't understand the complex interplay of models and data in weather forecasting, even though the previous chapter on baseball scouting is a perfect setup for it.  He misdescribes what a nonlinear equation is.  He misdescribes Ed Lorenz's famous chaos theory experiments.  By the end of Ch. 4, he can't even spell \\"television\\" correctly.  While he is complimentary of weather forecasters, he misses numerous connections with earlier chapters and really doesn't seem to understand the subject matter.  (At least he doesn't think weather forecasting = Al Roker.)<br /><br />But this is considerably better than his chapter on climate prediction (Ch. 12), in which he completely, utterly botches the definition of the greenhouse effect--he thinks it has to do with reflected solar radiation.  At that moment, he loses all credibility... it's as if he were a scientist discussing sabermetrics in the Baseball Research Journal and referred to a baseball as \\"the pigskin.\\"  Total fail.  (He cites a couple of URLs in this passage, including lecture notes from a Columbia/Barnard course I used to teach.  The quickie-Wiki approach to becoming an 'expert' in a field.  The URLs sound good, but clearly Silver learned zero about the greenhouse effect from them--zero.)  Then Silver sets up a false comparison between a discredited HeartlandInstitute forecaster and the worldwide efforts of the IPCC... fail.  He skims the whole world of climate modeling.  By chapter's end, he basically comes off as a college freshman out of his depth, writing a term paper the morning it's due.  He'd know more about climate forecasting if he read Ch. 16 of my college-level intro meteorology textbook, but apparently there was no time for anything that pedantic.<br /><br />Silver's Acknowledgments seem to reveal the problem: his research assistant seems to have been mostly responsible for the science parts of the book.  In retrospect, it's not hard to tell (from the depth of thinking and analysis, from the lack of proofreading, etc.) that Silver didn't care much about the science chapters in his book--a couple hours with a couple experts, some URLs provided by the researcher, that's plenty for him.  If Nate were in my freshman Intro to Weather and Climate class, he'd miss a lot of questions on the final exam.<br /><br />I like a lot of Silver's big-picture perspectives on forecasting, and his discussion of Bayesian statistics is illuminating, but his lack of follow-through in the scientific fields where forecasting has been explored most successfully has seriously undermined his book.  Somebody tell the New York Times that \\"The Signal and the Noise\\" is *not* one of the momentous books of the decade.  Instead, it's yet another example of a book that grew out of a blog but needed to stay in the oven a while longer.  Hey, Penguin Press, don't publishers hire expert content reviewers (not to mention copy editors) anymore?

Rise of the Guardians is a fun, behind-the-scenes look at the brotherhood of mythical creatures who protect and bring joy to children. In a way, their protection of kids is for their own good, too: they need the belief of kids in order to survive.<br /><br />There are fun moments in the movie: we learn that the Yeti make the toys at the North Pole, instead of elves. In a funny clip during the end credits, we even learn the one of the Yeti's given names is \\"Phil.\\" Sandman is one of the funniest characters in the film, despite being mute.<br /><br />There are also scary moments: The Boogieman sends nightmares into the world in an attempt to take away children's hope. He is visually striking: nearly monochromatic in an otherwise colorful world, and accompanied by swarms of black horses worthy of the Ringwraiths.<br /><br />*Spoiler Alert*<br /><br />There is hope: Children are able to defeat the Boogieman by facing and acknowledging frightening circumstances, and choosing not to be afraid of them. This is where the \\"wishful thinking\\" part of my title comes in. Bravery is a good virtue to commend to children, but some kids have been through traumatic circumstances in real life. They might not be able to just \\"choose\\" not to be afraid.<br /><br />*end of spoiler*<br /><br />Overall, Rise of the Guardians is a fun movie; it's worth your time, and will probably make your young kids laugh, shriek, and laugh again.
